href="/wiki/Khaemweset" title="Khaemweset">Khaemwaset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merneptah" title="Merneptah">Merneptah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meryatum" title="Meryatum">Meryatum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bintanath" class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em">Father</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Seti_I" title="Seti I">Seti I</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em">Mother</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Tuya_(queen)" title="Tuya (queen)">Tuya</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em">Died</th><td class="infobox-data">1213 BC (aged approximately 90)</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em">Burial</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/KV7" title="KV7">KV7</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em">Monuments</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abydos,_Egypt" title="Abydos, Egypt">Abydos</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ramesseum" title="Ramesseum">Ramesseum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bart_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bart-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karnak" title="Karnak">Karnak</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bart_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bart-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Ramesses II</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_language" title="Ancient Egyptian language">Ancient Egyptian</a>: <i lang="egy"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/r%EA%9C%A5-ms-sw" title="wikt:rꜥ-ms-sw">rꜥ-ms-sw</a></i> <i>Rīʿa-məsī-sū</i>, <small>pronounced </small><span class="IPA" lang="sem-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈɾiːʕaʔ məˈsiːˌsuw]</a></span>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> meaning "Ra is the one who bore him";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranke_1935_218_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ranke_1935_218-6">[6]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1303–1213 BC</span>), commonly known as <b>Ramesses the Great</b>, was the third <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Nineteenth Dynasty of Egypt">Nineteenth Dynasty of Egypt</a>. Along with <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_III" title="Thutmose III">Thutmose III</a> he is often regarded as the greatest, most celebrated, and most powerful pharaoh of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a>, itself the most powerful period of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPutnam1990[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_7-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPutnam1990[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-7'>[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <i>Ramesses</i> is pronounced variously <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Transliteration_of_Ancient_Egyptian" title="Transliteration of Ancient Egyptian">Other spellings</a> include <b>Rameses</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and <b>Ramses</b>; in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koin%C4%93_Greek_language" title="Koinē Greek language">Koinē Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ῥαμέσσης</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Koinē Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-x-koine-Latn">Rhaméssēs</i></span>. He is known as <b>Ozymandias</b> in Greek sources (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koin%C4%93_Greek_language" title="Koinē Greek language">Koinē Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ὀσυμανδύας</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Koinē Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-x-koine-Latn">Osymandýas</i></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> from the first part of Ramesses's <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a>, <span title="Ancient Egyptian-language text"><i lang="egy-Latn">Usermaatre <a href="/wiki/Setepenre" title="Setepenre">Setepenre</a></i></span>, "The <a href="/wiki/Maat" title="Maat">Maat</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a> is powerful, Chosen of Ra".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pdsOzymandias_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pdsOzymandias-11">[11]</a></sup> His successors and later Egyptians called him the "Great Ancestor".
</p><p>At age fourteen, he was appointed <a href="/wiki/Prince_regent" title="Prince regent">prince regent</a> by his father, <a href="/wiki/Seti_I" title="Seti I">Seti I</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPutnam1990[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_7-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPutnam1990[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-7'>[7]</a></sup> Most Egyptologists today believe he assumed the throne on 31 May 1279 BC, based on his known accession date of III <a href="/wiki/Season_of_the_Harvest" title="Season of the Harvest">Season of the Harvest</a>, day 27.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Beckerath1997108,_190_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Beckerath1997108,_190-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrand2000302–305_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrand2000302–305-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early part of his reign was focused on building cities, temples, and monuments. He established the city of <a href="/wiki/Pi-Ramesses" title="Pi-Ramesses">Pi-Ramesses</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> as his new capital and used it as the main base for his campaigns in <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syria</a>.  He led several military expeditions into the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, reasserting Egyptian control over <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>. He also led expeditions to the south, into <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, commemorated in inscriptions at <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Beit_el-Wali" title="Temple of Beit el-Wali">Beit el-Wali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerf_Hussein" title="Gerf Hussein">Gerf Hussein</a>.  He celebrated an unprecedented thirteen or fourteen <a href="/wiki/Sed_festival" title="Sed festival">Sed festivals</a> during his reign—more than any other pharaoh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'ConnorCline199816_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'ConnorCline199816-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates of his age at death vary; 90 or 91 is considered most likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Beckerath1997108,_190_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Beckerath1997108,_190-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrand2000302–305_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrand2000302–305-13">[13]</a></sup> On his death, he was buried in <a href="/wiki/KV7" title="KV7">a tomb</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Kings" title="Valley of the Kings">Valley of the Kings</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> his body was later moved to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DB320" title="DB320">royal cache</a> where it was discovered in 1881. It is now on display in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Egyptian_Civilization" title="National Museum of Egyptian Civilization">National Museum of Egyptian Civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parisse_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parisse-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_and_battles">Campaigns and battles</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg/220px-Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg/330px-Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg/440px-Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ramesses_II_as_child.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ramesses II as a child (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Museum,_Cairo" title="Egyptian Museum, Cairo">Egyptian Museum, Cairo</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Early in his life, Ramesses II embarked on numerous campaigns to restore possession of previously held territories lost to the <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> and to secure Egypt's borders. He was also responsible for suppressing some Nubian revolts and carrying out a campaign in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>. Though the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kadesh" title="Battle of Kadesh">Battle of Kadesh</a> often dominates the scholarly view of Ramesses II's military prowess and power, he nevertheless enjoyed more than a few outright victories over Egypt's enemies. During his reign, the Egyptian army is estimated to have totaled some 100,000 men: a formidable force that he used to strengthen Egyptian influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabriel6_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabriel6-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_against_Sherden_sea_pirates">Battle against Sherden sea pirates</span></h3>
<p>In his second year, Ramesses II decisively defeated the <a href="/wiki/Sherden" title="Sherden">Sherden</a> sea pirates who were wreaking havoc along Egypt's Mediterranean coast by attacking cargo-laden vessels travelling the sea routes to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992250–253_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992250–253-18">[18]</a></sup> The Sherden people probably came from the coast of <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, from southwest <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> or perhaps, also from the island of <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'ConnorCline2003112–113_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'ConnorCline2003112–113-21">[21]</a></sup> Ramesses posted troops and ships at strategic points along the coast and patiently allowed the pirates to attack their perceived prey before skillfully catching them by surprise in a sea battle and capturing them all in a single action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley200053_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley200053-22">[22]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanis,_Egypt" title="Tanis, Egypt">Tanis</a> speaks of their having come "in their war-ships from the midst of the sea, and none were able to stand before them". There probably was a naval battle somewhere near the mouth of the Nile, as shortly afterward, many Sherden are seen among the pharaoh's body-guard where they are conspicuous by their horned helmets having a ball projecting from the middle, their round shields, and the great <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_sword#Naue_II" title="Bronze Age sword">Naue II swords</a> with which they are depicted in inscriptions of the Battle of Kadesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In that sea battle, together with the Sherden, the pharaoh also defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lukka" title="Lukka">Lukka</a> (L'kkw, possibly the people later known as the <a href="/wiki/Lycians" title="Lycians">Lycians</a>), and the Šqrsšw (<a href="/wiki/Shekelesh" title="Shekelesh">Shekelesh</a>) peoples.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Syrian_campaign">First Syrian campaign</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_(9794491275).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="3676" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_%289794491275%29.jpg/220px-Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_%289794491275%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_%289794491275%29.jpg/330px-Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_%289794491275%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_%289794491275%29.jpg/440px-Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_%289794491275%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abu_Simbel_African_Captives_(9794491275).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>African prisoners in the temple Abu Simbel</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1133" data-file-width="967" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png/220px-Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png/330px-Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png/440px-Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ramses-ii-relief-from-memphis2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A relief of Ramses II from <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a> showing him capturing enemies: a Nubian, a Libyan and a Syrian, circa 1250 BC. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cairo_Museum" title="Cairo Museum">Cairo Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The immediate antecedents to the Battle of Kadesh were the early campaigns of Ramesses II into <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>. His first campaign seems to have taken place in the fourth year of his reign and was commemorated by the erection of what became the first of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commemorative_stelae_of_Nahr_el-Kalb" title="Commemorative stelae of Nahr el-Kalb">Commemorative stelae of Nahr el-Kalb</a> near what is now <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>. The inscription is almost totally illegible due to weathering.
</p><p>In the fourth year of his reign, he captured the Hittite vassal state of the Amurru during his campaign in Syria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1994253_ff_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1994253_ff-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Syrian_campaign">Second Syrian campaign</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kadesh" title="Battle of Kadesh">Battle of Kadesh</a></div>
<p>The Battle of Kadesh in his fifth regnal year was the climactic engagement in a campaign that Ramesses fought in Syria, against the resurgent Hittite forces of <a href="/wiki/Muwatalli_II" title="Muwatalli II">Muwatallis</a>. The pharaoh wanted a victory at Kadesh both to expand Egypt's frontiers into Syria, and to emulate his father Seti I's triumphal entry into the city just a decade or so earlier. He also constructed his new capital, <a href="/wiki/Pi-Ramesses" title="Pi-Ramesses">Pi-Ramesses</a>. There he built factories to manufacture weapons, chariots, and shields, supposedly producing some 1,000 weapons in a week, about 250 chariots in two weeks, and 1,000 shields in a week and a half. After these preparations, Ramesses moved to attack territory in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, which belonged to a more substantial enemy than any he had ever faced in war: the <a href="/wiki/Hittites#Geography" title="Hittites">Hittite Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley200068_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley200068-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ramesses's forces were caught in a Hittite ambush and outnumbered at Kadesh when they counterattacked and routed the Hittites, whose survivors abandoned their chariots and swam the Orontes river to reach the safe city walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HC20080514_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-HC20080514-27">[27]</a></sup> Ramesses, logistically unable to sustain a long siege, returned to Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hittites.info_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-hittites.info-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dougherty-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_Syrian_campaign">Third Syrian campaign</span></h3>
<p>Egypt's sphere of influence was now restricted to <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> while <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> fell into Hittite hands. Canaanite princes, seemingly encouraged by the Egyptian incapacity to impose their will and goaded on by the Hittites, began revolts against Egypt. In the seventh year of his reign, Ramesses II returned to Syria once again. This time he proved more successful against his Hittite foes. During this campaign he split his army into two forces. One force was led by his son, <a href="/wiki/Amun-her-khepeshef" title="Amun-her-khepeshef">Amun-her-khepeshef</a>, and it chased warriors of the <a href="/wiki/Shasu" title="Shasu">Šhasu</a> tribes across the <a href="/wiki/Negev" title="Negev">Negev</a> as far as the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>, capturing <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">Edom</a>-<a href="/wiki/Mount_Seir" title="Mount Seir">Seir</a>. It then marched on to capture <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">Moab</a>. The other force, led by Ramesses, attacked <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a>. He, too, then entered Moab, where he rejoined his son. The reunited army then marched on <a href="/wiki/Heshbon" title="Heshbon">Hesbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, on to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kumidi" title="Kumidi">Kumidi</a>, and finally, recaptured Upi (the land around Damascus), reestablishing Egypt's former sphere of influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992256_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992256-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_campaigns_in_Syria">Later campaigns in Syria</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dapur" title="Siege of Dapur">Siege of Dapur</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1846" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg/220px-Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg/330px-Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg/440px-Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ramses_II_besieging_the_Cheta_people_in_Dapur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Color reproduction of the relief depicting Ramesses II storming the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> fortress of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dapur" title="Siege of Dapur">Dapur</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ramesses extended his military successes in his eighth and ninth years. He crossed the Dog River (<a href="/wiki/Nahr_al-Kalb" title="Nahr al-Kalb">Nahr al-Kalb</a>) and pushed north into Amurru. His armies managed to march as far north as Dapur,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199626_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199626-31">[31]</a></sup> where he had a statue of himself erected. The Egyptian pharaoh thus found himself in northern Amurru, well past <a href="/wiki/Kadesh_(Syria)" title="Kadesh (Syria)">Kadesh</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Tunip" title="Tunip">Tunip</a>, where no Egyptian soldier had been seen since the time of <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_III" title="Thutmose III">Thutmose III</a>, almost 120 years earlier. He laid siege to the city before capturing it. His victory proved to be ephemeral. In year nine, Ramesses erected a stele at <a href="/wiki/Beit_She%27an" title="Beit She'an">Beth Shean</a>. After having reasserted his power over Canaan, Ramesses led his army north. A mostly illegible stele near <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, which appears to be dated to the king's second year, was probably set up there in his tenth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen1979223–224_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen1979223–224-32">[32]</a></sup> The thin strip of territory pinched between Amurru and Kadesh did not make for a stable possession. Within a year, they had returned to the Hittite fold, so that Ramesses had to march against <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dapur" title="Siege of Dapur">Dapur</a> once more in his tenth year. This time he claimed to have fought the battle without even bothering to put on his <a href="/wiki/Corslet" title="Corslet">corslet</a>, until two hours after the fighting began. Six of Ramesses's youthful sons, still wearing their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Youth_side_lock" title="Youth side lock">side locks</a>, took part in this conquest. He took towns in <a href="/wiki/Retjenu" title="Retjenu">Retjenu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199633_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199633-33">[33]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tunip" title="Tunip">Tunip</a> in <a href="/wiki/Naharin" title="Naharin">Naharin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199647_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199647-34">[34]</a></sup> later recorded on the walls of the Ramesseum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199646_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199646-35">[35]</a></sup> This second success at the location was equally as meaningless as his first, as neither power could decisively defeat the other in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen198268_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen198268-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:662px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1591" data-file-width="7112" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg/660px-West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg/990px-West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg/1320px-West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg 2x" width="660"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_Asiatic_prisoners_of_Ramses_II_at_Abu_Simbel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>West Asiatic prisoners of Ramses II at <a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_treaty_with_the_Hittites">Peace treaty with the Hittites</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian%E2%80%93Hittite_peace_treaty" title="Egyptian–Hittite peace treaty">Egyptian–Hittite peace treaty</a></div>
<p>The deposed Hittite king, <a href="/wiki/Mursili_III" title="Mursili III">Mursili III</a>, fled to Egypt, the land of his country's enemy, after the failure of his plots to oust his uncle from the throne. <a href="/wiki/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1ili_III" title="Ḫattušili III">Ḫattušili III</a> responded by demanding that Ramesses II extradite his nephew back to <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hatti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen198274_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen198274-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3617" data-file-width="2154" decoding="async" height="369" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg/220px-Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg/330px-Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg/440px-Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Treaty_of_Kadesh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tablet of <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> between <a href="/wiki/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1ili_III" title="Ḫattušili III">Ḫattušili III</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hatti</a> and Ramesses II of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%B0stanbul_Archaeology_Museums" title="İstanbul Archaeology Museums">İstanbul Archaeology Museums</a></div></div></div>
<p>This demand precipitated a crisis in relations between Egypt and Hatti when Ramesses denied any knowledge of Mursili's whereabouts in his country, and the two empires came dangerously close to war. Eventually, in the twenty-first year of his reign (1258 BC), Ramesses decided to conclude an agreement with the new Hittite king, Ḫattušili III, at Kadesh to end the conflict. The ensuing document is the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Peace_treaty" title="Peace treaty">peace treaty</a> in world history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992256_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992256-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg/220px-Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg/330px-Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg/440px-Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kolosstatue_Ramses_II_Memphis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kolosstatue Ramses II Memphis</div></div></div>
<p>The peace treaty was recorded in two versions, one in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a>, the other in Hittite, using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a>; both versions survive. Such dual-language recording is common to many subsequent treaties. This treaty differs from others, in that the two language versions are worded differently. While the majority of the text is identical, the Hittite version says the Egyptians came suing for peace and the Egyptian version says the reverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen198362–64,_73–79_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen198362–64,_73–79-39">[39]</a></sup> The treaty was given to the Egyptians in the form of a silver plaque, and this "pocket-book" version was taken back to Egypt and carved into the temple at <a href="/wiki/Karnak" title="Karnak">Karnak</a>.
</p><p>The treaty was concluded between Ramesses II and Ḫattušili III in year 21 of Ramesses's reign (c. 1258 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992257_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992257-40">[40]</a></sup> Its 18 articles call for peace between Egypt and Hatti and then proceeds to maintain that their respective deities also demand peace. The frontiers are not laid down in this treaty, but may be inferred from other documents. The Anastasy A <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> describes <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> during the latter part of the reign of Ramesses II and enumerates and names the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> coastal towns under Egyptian control. The harbour town of <a href="/wiki/Sumur_(Levant)" title="Sumur (Levant)">Sumur</a>, north of <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>, is mentioned as the northernmost town belonging to Egypt, suggesting it contained an Egyptian garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz199145_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz199145-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>No further Egyptian campaigns in Canaan are mentioned after the conclusion of the peace treaty. The northern border seems to have been safe and quiet, so the rule of the pharaoh was strong until Ramesses II's death, and the waning of the dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen1982215_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen1982215-42">[42]</a></sup> When the King of Mira attempted to involve Ramesses in a hostile act against the Hittites, the Egyptian responded that the times of intrigue in support of Mursili III, had passed. Ḫattušili III wrote to <a href="/wiki/Kadashman-Enlil_II" title="Kadashman-Enlil II">Kadashman-Enlil II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite</a> king of <a href="/wiki/Kardunia%C5%A1" title="Karduniaš">Karduniaš</a> (<a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>) in the same spirit, reminding him of the time when his father, <a href="/wiki/Kadashman-Turgu" title="Kadashman-Turgu">Kadashman-Turgu</a>, had offered to fight Ramesses II, the king of Egypt. The Hittite king encouraged the Babylonian to oppose another enemy, which must have been the king of <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, whose allies had killed the messenger of the Egyptian king. Ḫattušili encouraged Kadashman-Enlil to come to his aid and prevent the Assyrians from cutting the link between the Canaanite province of Egypt and Mursili III, the ally of Ramesses.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_in_Nubia">Campaigns in Nubia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gerf_Hussein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="909" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Gerf_Hussein.jpg/220px-Gerf_Hussein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Gerf_Hussein.jpg/330px-Gerf_Hussein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Gerf_Hussein.jpg/440px-Gerf_Hussein.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gerf_Hussein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalabsha#Gerf_Hussein" title="Kalabsha">Gerf Hussein</a> temple, originally in Nubia</div></div></div>
<p>Ramesses II also campaigned south of the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">first cataract of the Nile</a> into <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>. When Ramesses was about 22, two of his own sons, including <a href="/wiki/Amun-her-khepeshef" title="Amun-her-khepeshef">Amun-her-khepeshef</a>, accompanied him in at least one of those campaigns. By the time of Ramesses, Nubia had been a colony for 200 years, but its conquest was recalled in decoration from the temples Ramesses II built at <a href="/wiki/New_Kalabsha#Beit_al-Wali" title="New Kalabsha">Beit el-Wali</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> (which was the subject of epigraphic work by the <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Institute_(Chicago)" title="Oriental Institute (Chicago)">Oriental Institute</a> during the Nubian salvage campaign of the 1960s),<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTERickeHughesWente1967[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_44-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTERickeHughesWente1967[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-44'>[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Kalabsha#Gerf_Hussein" title="New Kalabsha">Gerf Hussein</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalabsha" title="Kalabsha">Kalabsha</a> in northern Nubia. On the south wall of the Beit el-Wali temple, Ramesses II is depicted charging into battle against tribes south of Egypt in a war chariot, while his two young sons, Amun-her-khepsef and Khaemwaset, are shown behind him, also in war chariots. A wall in one of Ramesses's temples says he had to fight one battle with those tribes without help from his soldiers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_(Plaster_Cast),_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg" title="Image: 800 pixels"><img alt="Image: 800 pixels" data-file-height="1796" data-file-width="6392" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_%28Plaster_Cast%29%2C_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg/800px-Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_%28Plaster_Cast%29%2C_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_%28Plaster_Cast%29%2C_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg/1200px-Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_%28Plaster_Cast%29%2C_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_%28Plaster_Cast%29%2C_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg/1600px-Wall_Painting_of_Temple_of_Beit_El-Wali_%28Plaster_Cast%29%2C_which_Ramses_II_Constructed_in_Nubia_-_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_in_Libya">Campaigns in Libya</span></h3>
<p>During the reign of Ramesses II, the Egyptians were evidently active on a 300-kilometre (190 mi) stretch along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> coast, at least as far as <a href="/wiki/Zawyet_Umm_El_Rakham" title="Zawyet Umm El Rakham">Zawyet Umm El Rakham</a>, where remains of a fortress described by its texts as built on Libyans land have been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Although the exact events surrounding the foundation of the coastal forts and fortresses is not clear, some degree of political and military control must have been held over the region to allow their construction. 
</p><p>There are no detailed accounts of Ramesses II's undertaking large military actions against the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyans</a>, only generalised records of his conquering and crushing them, which may or may not refer to specific events that were otherwise unrecorded. It may be that some of the records, such as the <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a> Stele of his year 2, are harking back to Ramesses's presence on his father's Libyan campaigns. Perhaps it was <a href="/wiki/Seti_I" title="Seti I">Seti I</a> who achieved this supposed control over the region, and who planned to establish the defensive system, in a manner similar to how he rebuilt those to the east, the Ways of Horus across Northern <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sed_festival">Sed festival</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sed_festival" title="Sed festival">Sed festival</a></div>
<p>After reigning for 30 years, Ramesses joined a select group that included only a handful of Egypt's longest-lived rulers. By tradition, in the 30th year of his reign Ramesses celebrated a jubilee called the <a href="/wiki/Sed_festival" title="Sed festival">Sed festival</a>. These were held to honour and rejuvenate the pharaoh's strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEMHedSed_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-GEMHedSed-46">[46]</a></sup> Only halfway through what would be a 66-year reign, Ramesses had already eclipsed all but a few of his greatest predecessors in his achievements. He had brought peace, maintained Egyptian borders, and built great and numerous monuments across the empire. His country was more prosperous and powerful than it had been in nearly a century.
</p><p>Sed festivals traditionally were held again every three years after the 30th year; Ramesses II, who sometimes held them after two years, eventually celebrated an unprecedented thirteen or fourteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_activity_and_monuments">Building activity and monuments</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
