<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_(Turin)_(2865505031).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1101" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_%28Turin%29_%282865505031%29.jpg/220px-Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_%28Turin%29_%282865505031%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_%28Turin%29_%282865505031%29.jpg/330px-Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_%28Turin%29_%282865505031%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_%28Turin%29_%282865505031%29.jpg/440px-Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_%28Turin%29_%282865505031%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_mus%C3%A9e_%C3%A9gyptien_(Turin)_(2865505031).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ramesses II with <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mut" title="Mut">Mut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Museo_Egizio" title="Museo Egizio">Museo Egizio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the third year of his reign, Ramesses started the most ambitious building project after the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">pyramids</a>, which were built almost 1,500 years earlier. The population was put to work changing the face of Egypt. Ramesses built extensively from the <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">Delta</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, "covering the land with buildings in a way no monarch before him had."<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestendorf1969[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_48-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestendorf1969[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-48'>[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_(10).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3432" data-file-width="2576" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_%2810%29.jpg/220px-Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_%2810%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_%2810%29.jpg/330px-Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_%2810%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_%2810%29.jpg/440px-Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_%2810%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luxor_Temple_-_panoramio_(10).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colossal Statue of Ramses II in the first peristyle court at <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a></div></div></div>
<p>Some of the activities undertaken were focused on remodeling or usurping existing works, improving masonry techniques, and using art as propaganda.
</p>
<ul><li>In Thebes, the ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_temple" title="Egyptian temple">temples</a> were transformed, so that each one of them reflected honour to Ramesses as a symbol of his putative divine nature and power.</li>
<li>The elegant but shallow reliefs of previous pharaohs were easily transformed, and so their images and words could easily be obliterated by their successors. Ramesses insisted that his carvings be deeply engraved into the stone, which made them not only less susceptible to later alteration, but also made them more prominent in the Egyptian sun, reflecting his relationship with the sun deity, <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>.</li>
<li>Ramesses used art as a means of propaganda for his victories over foreigners, which are depicted on numerous temple reliefs.</li>
<li>His <a href="/wiki/Cartouche" title="Cartouche">cartouches</a> are prominently displayed even in buildings that he did not construct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li>He also founded a new capital city in the Delta during his reign, called <a href="/wiki/Pi-Ramesses" title="Pi-Ramesses">Pi-Ramesses</a>. It previously had served as a summer palace during Seti I's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen1982119_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen1982119-50">[50]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Ramesses also undertook many new construction projects.   Two of his biggest works, besides <a href="/wiki/Pi-Ramesses" title="Pi-Ramesses">Pi-Ramesses</a>, were the temple complex of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ramesseum" title="Ramesseum">Ramesseum</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mortuary_temple" title="Mortuary temple">mortuary temple</a> in  western <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pi-Ramesses">Pi-Ramesses</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pi-Ramesses" title="Pi-Ramesses">Pi-Ramesses</a></div>
<p>Ramesses II moved the capital of his kingdom from Thebes in the Nile valley to a new site in the eastern Delta. His motives are uncertain, although he possibly wished to be closer to his territories in Canaan and Syria. The new city of Pi-Ramesses (or to give the full name, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Per_(hieroglyph)" title="Per (hieroglyph)">Pi</a>-Ramesses Aa-nakhtu</i>, meaning "Domain of Ramesses, Great in Victory")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2003255_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2003255-51">[51]</a></sup> was dominated by huge temples and his vast residential palace, complete with its own zoo. In the 10th century AD the Bible exegete Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">Saadia Gaon</a>, believed that the biblical site of Ramesses had to be identified with <a href="/wiki/Ain_Shams" title="Ain Shams">Ain Shams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> For a time, during the early 20th century, the site was misidentified as that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanis,_Egypt" title="Tanis, Egypt">Tanis</a>, due to the amount of statuary and other material from Pi-Ramesses found there, but it now is recognized that the Ramesside remains at Tanis were brought there from elsewhere, and the real Pi-Ramesses lies about 30 km (18.6 mi) south, near modern <a href="/wiki/Qantir" title="Qantir">Qantir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The colossal feet of the statue of Ramesses are almost all that remains above ground today. The rest is buried in the fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2003255_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2003255-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ramesseum">Ramesseum</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ramesseum" title="Ramesseum">Ramesseum</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="555" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/92/Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg/220px-Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/92/Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg/330px-Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/92/Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg/440px-Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ramesses_II_at_BM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Younger_Memnon" title="Younger Memnon">Younger Memnon</a>: part of colossal statue of Ramesses from <a href="/wiki/Ramesseum" title="Ramesseum">Ramesseum</a>, now in <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The temple complex built by Ramesses II between <a href="/wiki/Kurna" title="Kurna">Qurna</a> and the desert has been known as the <a href="/wiki/Ramesseum" title="Ramesseum">Ramesseum</a> since the 19th century. The Greek <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> marveled at the gigantic temple, now no more than a few ruins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oriented northwest and southeast, the temple was preceded by two courts. An enormous pylon stood before the first court, with the royal palace at the left and the gigantic statue of the king looming up at the back. Only fragments of the base and torso remain of the <a href="/wiki/Syenite" title="Syenite">syenite</a> statue of the enthroned pharaoh, 17 metres (56 ft) high and weighing more than 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (980 <a href="/wiki/Long_ton" title="Long ton">long tons</a>; 1,100 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>). Scenes of the great pharaoh and his army triumphing over the Hittite forces fleeing before Kadesh are represented on the pylon. Remains of the second court include part of the internal facade of the pylon and a portion of the Osiride portico on the right. Scenes of war and the alleged rout of the Hittites at Kadesh are repeated on the walls. In the upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Register_(sculpture)" title="Register (sculpture)">registers</a>, feast and honor of the phallic deity <a href="/wiki/Min_(god)" title="Min (god)">Min</a>, god of fertility.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2528" data-file-width="3636" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg/220px-Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg/330px-Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg/440px-Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luxor_Ramesseum_R03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scattered remains displayed in front of <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osirid</a> statues</div></div></div>
<p>On the opposite side of the court the few Osiride pillars and columns still remaining may furnish an idea of the original grandeur.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkliar2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_55-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkliar2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-55'>[55]</a></sup> Scattered remains of the two statues of the seated king also may be seen, one in pink granite and the other in black granite, which once flanked the entrance to the temple. Thirty-nine out of the forty-eight columns in the great <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypostyle_hall" title="Hypostyle hall">hypostyle hall</a> (41 × 31 m) still stand in the central rows. They are decorated with the usual scenes of the king before various deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramesseum-2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ramesseum-2-56">[56]</a></sup> Part of the ceiling, decorated with gold stars on a blue ground, also has been preserved. Ramesses's children appear in the procession on the few walls left. The sanctuary was composed of three consecutive rooms, with eight columns and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrastyle" title="Tetrastyle">tetrastyle</a> cell. Part of the first room, with the ceiling decorated with astral scenes, and few remains of the second room are all that is left. Vast storerooms built of mud bricks stretched out around the temple.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkliar2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_55-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkliar2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-55'>[55]</a></sup> Traces of a school for scribes were found among the ruins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>A temple of <a href="/wiki/Seti_I" title="Seti I">Seti I</a>, of which nothing remains beside the foundations, once stood to the right of the hypostyle hall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramesseum-2_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-ramesseum-2-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abu_Simbel">Abu Simbel</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel_temples" title="Abu Simbel temples">Abu Simbel temples</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_%282%29.jpg/220px-Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_%282%29.jpg/330px-Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_%282%29.jpg/440px-Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temple_d%27Abu_Simbel_-_panoramio_-_youssef_alam_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Facade of the Great Temple at <a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1255 BC, Ramesses and his queen <a href="/wiki/Nefertari" title="Nefertari">Nefertari</a> had traveled into <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> to inaugurate a new temple, the great <a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a>. It is an ego cast in stone; the man who built it intended not only to become Egypt's greatest pharaoh, but also one of its deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen198264–65_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen198264–65-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The great temple of Ramesses II at Abu Simbel was discovered in 1813 by the Swiss Orientalist and traveler <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Burckhardt" title="Johann Ludwig Burckhardt">Johann Ludwig Burckhardt</a>. An enormous pile of sand almost completely covered the facade and its colossal statues, blocking the entrance for four more years. The <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Paduan</a> explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Belzoni" title="Giovanni Battista Belzoni">Giovanni Battista Belzoni</a> reached the interior on 4 August 1817.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiliotti1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_59-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiliotti1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-59'>[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Nubian_monuments">Other Nubian monuments</span></h3>
<p>As well as the temples of Abu Simbel, Ramesses left other monuments to himself in Nubia. His early campaigns are illustrated on the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Beit_el-Wali" title="Temple of Beit el-Wali">Temple of Beit el-Wali</a> (now relocated to <a href="/wiki/New_Kalabsha" title="New Kalabsha">New Kalabsha</a>). Other temples dedicated to Ramesses are <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Derr" title="Temple of Derr">Derr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerf_Hussein" title="Gerf Hussein">Gerf Hussein</a> (also relocated to New Kalabsha).
For the temple of Amun at <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Barkal" title="Jebel Barkal">Jebel Barkal</a>, the temple's foundation probably occurred during the reign of Thutmose III, while the temple was shaped during his reign and that of Ramses II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archeological_discoveries">Archeological discoveries</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colossal_statue">Colossal statue</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Ramesses_II" title="Statue of Ramesses II">Statue of Ramesses II</a></div>
<p>The colossal <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Ramesses_II" title="Statue of Ramesses II">statue of Ramesses II</a> dates back 3,200 years, and was originally discovered in six pieces in a temple near <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>.  Weighing some  83-tonne (82-long-ton; 91-short-ton), it was transported, reconstructed, and erected in Ramesses Square in Cairo in 1955. In August 2006, contractors relocated it to save it from exhaust fumes that were causing it to deteriorate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>  The new site is near the future <a href="/wiki/Grand_Egyptian_Museum" title="Grand Egyptian Museum">Grand Egyptian Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColossalHawass_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColossalHawass-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Festival_chair">Festival chair</span></h4>
<p>In 2018, a group of archeologists in Cairo's Matariya neighborhood discovered pieces of a booth with a seat that, based on its structure and age, may have been used by Ramesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cairo_2018_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cairo_2018-64">[64]</a></sup> "The royal compartment consists of four steps leading to a cubic platform, which is believed to be the base of the king's seat during celebrations or public gatherings," such as Ramesses' inauguration and Sed festivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cairo_2018_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cairo_2018-64">[64]</a></sup> It may have also gone on to be used by others in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramesside_Period" title="Ramesside Period">Ramesside Period</a>, according to the mission's head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cairo_2018_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cairo_2018-64">[64]</a></sup> The excavation mission also unearthed "a collection of scarabs, amulets, clay pots and blocks engraved with hieroglyphic text."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cairo_2018_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cairo_2018-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Granite_bust">Granite bust</span></h4>
<p>In December 2019, a red granite royal bust of Ramesses II was unearthed by an Egyptian archaeological mission in the village of Mit Rahina in Giza. The bust depicted Ramesses II wearing a wig with the symbol "Ka" on his head. Its measurements were 55 cm (21.65 in) wide, 45 cm (17.71 in) thick and 105 cm (41.33 in) long. Alongside the bust, limestone blocks appeared showing Ramesses II during the <a href="/wiki/Sed_festival" title="Sed festival">Heb-Sed</a> religious ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> "This discovery is considered one of the rarest archaeological discoveries. It is the first-ever Ka statue made of granite to be discovered. The only Ka statue that was previously found is made of wood and it belongs to one of the kings of the 13th dynasty of ancient Egypt which is displayed at the Egyptian Museum in Tahrir Square," said archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Mostafa_Waziri" title="Mostafa Waziri">Mostafa Waziri</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_burial">Death and burial</span></h2>
<p>The Egyptian scholar <a href="/wiki/Manetho" title="Manetho">Manetho</a> (third century BC) attributed Ramesses a reign of 66 years and 2 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the time of his death, aged about 90 years, Ramesses was suffering from severe dental problems and was plagued by <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">hardening of the arteries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LMD_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-LMD-67">[67]</a></sup> He had made Egypt rich from all the supplies and bounty he had collected from other empires. He had outlived many of his wives and children and left great memorials all over <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Nine more pharaohs took the name Ramesses in his honour.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mummy">Mummy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/KV7" title="KV7">KV7</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ramses2_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="598" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Ramses2_%282%29.jpg/170px-Ramses2_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Ramses2_%282%29.jpg/255px-Ramses2_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Ramses2_%282%29.jpg/340px-Ramses2_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ramses2_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mummy of Ramesses II, now in the Cairo Museum</div></div></div>
<p>Originally Ramesses II was buried in the tomb <a href="/wiki/KV7" title="KV7">KV7</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madaintomb_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-madaintomb-68">[68]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Kings" title="Valley of the Kings">Valley of the Kings</a>, but because of looting, priests later transferred the body to a holding area, re-wrapped it, and placed it inside the tomb of queen <a href="/wiki/Ahmose_Inhapy" title="Ahmose Inhapy">Ahmose Inhapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERohl199572–73,_75_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERohl199572–73,_75-69">[69]</a></sup> Seventy-two hours later it was again moved, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DB320" title="DB320">tomb</a> of the high priest <a href="/wiki/Pinedjem_II" title="Pinedjem II">Pinedjem II</a>. All of this is recorded in hieroglyphics on the linen covering the body of the coffin of Ramesses II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERohl199578–79_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERohl199578–79-70">[70]</a></sup> His mummy was eventually discovered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TT320" title="TT320">TT320</a> inside an ordinary wooden coffin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MadainMummy_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-MadainMummy-71">[71]</a></sup> and is now in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Egyptian_Civilization" title="National Museum of Egyptian Civilization">National Museum of Egyptian Civilization</a> (until 3 April 2021 it was in the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Museum" title="Egyptian Museum">Egyptian Museum</a>).
</p><p>The pharaoh's mummy reveals an <a href="/wiki/Aquiline_nose" title="Aquiline nose">aquiline nose</a> and strong jaw. It stands at about 1.7 metres (5 ft 7 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley200014_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley200014-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Maspero" title="Gaston Maspero">Gaston Maspero</a>, who first unwrapped the mummy of Ramesses II, writes, "on the temples there are a few sparse hairs, but at the poll the hair is quite thick, forming smooth, straight locks about five centimeters in length. White at the time of death, and possibly auburn during life, they have been dyed a light red by the spices (henna) used in embalming...the moustache and beard are thin...The hairs are white, like those of the head and eyebrows...the skin is of earthy brown, splotched with black... the face of the mummy gives a fair idea of the face of the living king."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1975, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Bucaille" title="Maurice Bucaille">Maurice Bucaille</a>, a French doctor, examined the mummy at the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Museum" title="Egyptian Museum">Cairo Museum</a> and found it in poor condition. French President <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9ry_Giscard_d%27Estaing" title="Valéry Giscard d'Estaing">Valéry Giscard d'Estaing</a> succeeded in convincing Egyptian authorities to send the mummy to France for treatment. In September 1976, it was greeted at <a href="/wiki/Paris%E2%80%93Le_Bourget_Airport" title="Paris–Le Bourget Airport">Paris–Le Bourget Airport</a> with full military honours befitting a king, then taken to a laboratory at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Homme" title="Musée de l'Homme">Musée de l'Homme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2688" data-file-width="3584" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG/220px-Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG/330px-Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG/440px-Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mummy_of_Ramesses_II_-_02.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mummy of Ramesses the Great</div></div></div>
<p>The mummy was forensically tested by Pierre-Fernand Ceccaldi, the chief forensic scientist at the Criminal Identification Laboratory of Paris. Ceccaldi observed that the mummy had slightly wavy, red hair; from this trait combined with cranial features, he concluded that Ramesses II was of a "Berber type" and hence – according to Ceccaldi's <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">"race"-based</a> analysis – fair-skinned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Subsequent microscopic inspection of the roots of Ramesses II's hair proved that the king's hair originally was red, which suggests that he came from a family of redheads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley2001[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidhzbRBN6Ugr0Cq22redhair22_??]_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley2001[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidhzbRBN6Ugr0Cq22redhair22_??]-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrier1994153_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrier1994153-81">[81]</a></sup> This has more than just cosmetic significance: in ancient Egypt people with red hair were associated with the deity <a href="/wiki/Set_(deity)" title="Set (deity)">Set</a>, the slayer of <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a>, and the name of Ramesses II's father, Seti I, means "follower of Seth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrier1994200–201_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrier1994200–201-82">[82]</a></sup> However, Diop disputes the results of the study and argues that the structure of hair morphology cannot determine the ethnicity of a mummy and that a comparative study should have featured Nubians in Upper Egypt before a conclusive judgement was reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980, James Harris and <a href="/wiki/Edward_F._Wente" title="Edward F. Wente">Edward F. Wente</a> conducted a series of X-ray examinations on New Kingdom Pharaohs crania and skeletal remains, which included the mummified remains of Ramesses II. The analysis in general found strong similarities between the New Kingdom rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Nineteenth Dynasty of Egypt">19th Dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twentieth Dynasty of Egypt">20th Dynasty</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> Nubian samples. The authors also noted affinities with modern Mediterranean populations of Levantine origin. Harris and Wente suggested this represented admixture as the Rammessides were of northern origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the examination, scientific analysis revealed battle-wounds, old fractures, <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> and poor circulation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Ramesses II's arthritis is believed to have made him walk with a hunched back for the last decades of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrier1998153_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrier1998153-85">[85]</a></sup> A 2004 study excluded <a href="/wiki/Ankylosing_spondylitis" title="Ankylosing spondylitis">ankylosing spondylitis</a> as a possible cause and proposed diffuse idiopathic skeletal hyperostosis as a possible alternative,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> which was confirmed by more recent work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> A significant hole in the pharaoh's <a href="/wiki/Mandible" title="Mandible">mandible</a> was detected. Researchers observed "an abscess by his teeth (which) was serious enough to have caused death by infection, although this cannot be determined with certainty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrier1998153_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrier1998153-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>After being irradiated in an attempt to eliminate fungi and insects, the mummy was returned from Paris to Egypt in May 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2021 his mummy was moved from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_Egyptian_Antiquities" title="Museum of Egyptian Antiquities">Museum of Egyptian Antiquities</a> to the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Egyptian_Civilization" title="National Museum of Egyptian Civilization">National Museum of Egyptian Civilization</a> along with those of 17 other kings and 4 queens in an event termed the <a href="/wiki/Pharaohs%27_Golden_Parade" title="Pharaohs' Golden Parade">Pharaohs' Golden Parade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parisse_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parisse-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Spouse_and_relatives.27_burials"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spouse_and_relatives'_burials">Spouse and relatives' burials</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomb_of_Nefertari">Tomb of Nefertari</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QV66" title="QV66">Tomb of Nefertari</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2320" data-file-width="1576" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg/220px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg/330px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg/440px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_der_Nefertari_004_retouched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb wall depicting <a href="/wiki/Nefertari" title="Nefertari">Nefertari</a></div></div></div>
<p>The tomb of the most important <a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">consort</a> of Ramesses was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Ernesto_Schiaparelli" title="Ernesto Schiaparelli">Ernesto Schiaparelli</a> in 1904.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkliar2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_55-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkliar2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-55'>[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiliotti1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_59-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiliotti1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-59'>[59]</a></sup> Although it had been looted in ancient times, the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Nefertari" title="Nefertari">Nefertari</a> is extremely important, because its magnificent wall-painting decoration is regarded as one of the greatest achievements of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_of_Ancient_Egypt" title="Art of Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptian art</a>. A flight of steps cut out of the rock gives access to the antechamber, which is decorated with paintings based on chapter seventeen of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Dead" title="Book of the Dead">Book of the Dead</a>. This astronomical ceiling represents the heavens and is painted in dark blue, with a myriad of golden five-pointed stars. The east wall of the antechamber is interrupted by a large opening flanked by representation of <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a> at the left and <a href="/wiki/Anubis" title="Anubis">Anubis</a> at the right; this in turn leads to the side chamber, decorated with offering-scenes, preceded by a vestibule in which the paintings portray Nefertari presented to the deities, who welcome her. On the north wall of the antechamber is the stairway down to the burial-chamber, a vast quadrangular room covering a surface-area of about 90 square metres (970 sq ft), its astronomical ceiling supported by four pillars, entirely decorated. Originally, the queen's red granite <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> lay in the middle of this chamber. According to religious doctrines of the time, it was in this chamber, which the ancient Egyptians called the Golden Hall, that the regeneration of the deceased took place. This decorative pictogram of the walls in the burial-chamber drew inspiration from chapters 144 and 146 of the Book of the Dead: in the left half of the chamber, there are passages from chapter 144 concerning the gates and doors of the kingdom of Osiris, their guardians, and the magic formulas that had to be uttered by the deceased in order to go past the doors.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiliotti1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_59-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiliotti1994[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-59'>[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomb_KV5">Tomb KV5</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/KV5" title="KV5">KV5</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_children_of_Ramesses_II" title="List of children of Ramesses II">List of children of Ramesses II</a></div>
<p>In 1995, Professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kent_Weeks" title="Kent Weeks">Kent Weeks</a>, head of the Theban Mapping Project, rediscovered Tomb <a href="/wiki/KV5" title="KV5">KV5</a>. It has proven to be the largest tomb in the Valley of the Kings, and originally contained the mummified remains of some of this king's estimated 52 sons. Approximately 150 corridors and tomb chambers have been located in this tomb as of 2006 and the tomb may contain as many as 200 corridors and chambers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> It is believed that at least four of Ramesses's sons, including Meryatum, Sety, <a href="/wiki/Amun-her-khepeshef" title="Amun-her-khepeshef">Amun-her-khepeshef</a> (Ramesses's first-born son) and "the King's Principal Son of His Body, the Generalissimo Ramesses, justified" (i.e., deceased) were buried there from inscriptions, <a href="/wiki/Ostracon" title="Ostracon">ostraca</a> or <a href="/wiki/Canopic_jar" title="Canopic jar">canopic jars</a> discovered in the tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley2000161–162_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley2000161–162-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Tyldesley" title="Joyce Tyldesley">Joyce Tyldesley</a> writes that thus far
</p>
<dl><dd>no intact burials have been discovered and there have been little substantial funeral debris: thousands of potsherds, faience <i>ushabti</i> figures, beads, amulets, fragments of Canopic jars, of wooden coffins ... but no intact sarcophagi, mummies or <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">mummy</a> cases, suggesting that much of the tomb may have been unused. Those burials which were made in KV5 were thoroughly looted in antiquity, leaving little or no remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley2000161–162_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley2000161–162-90">[90]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_the_pharaoh_of_the_Exodus">As the pharaoh of the Exodus</span></h2>
<p>Ramesses II is one of the more popular candidates for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharaoh_of_the_Exodus" title="Pharaoh of the Exodus">Pharaoh of the Exodus</a>. He is cast in this role in the 1944 novella <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tables_of_the_Law" title="The Tables of the Law">The Tables of the Law</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>. Although not a major character, Ramesses appears in <a href="/wiki/Joan_Grant" title="Joan Grant">Joan Grant</a>'s <i>So Moses Was Born</i>, a first-person account from Nebunefer, the brother of Ramose, which paints a picture of the life of Ramose from the death of Seti, replete with the power play, intrigue, and assassination plots of the historical record, and depicting the relationships with <a href="/wiki/Bintanath" title="Bintanath">Bintanath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuya_(queen)" title="Tuya (queen)">Tuya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nefertari" title="Nefertari">Nefertari</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>.
</p><p>In film, Ramesses is played by <a href="/wiki/Yul_Brynner" title="Yul Brynner">Yul Brynner</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>'s classic <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1956_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1956 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i> (1956). Here Ramesses is portrayed as a vengeful tyrant as well as the main antagonist of the film, ever scornful of his father's preference for Moses over "the son of [his] body".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>  The animated film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince_of_Egypt" title="The Prince of Egypt">The Prince of Egypt</a></i> (1998) also features a depiction of Ramesses (voiced by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Fiennes" title="Ralph Fiennes">Ralph Fiennes</a>, for both the speaking and the singing), portrayed as Moses' adoptive brother, and ultimately as the film's villain with essentially the same motivations as in the earlier 1956 film. <a href="/wiki/Joel_Edgerton" title="Joel Edgerton">Joel Edgerton</a> played Ramesses in the 2014 film <i><a href="/wiki/Exodus:_Gods_and_Kings" title="Exodus: Gods and Kings">Exodus: Gods and Kings</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%A9rgio_Marone" title="Sérgio Marone">Sérgio Marone</a> plays Ramesses in the 2015–2016 Brazilian telenovela series <i><a href="/wiki/Os_Dez_Mandamentos" title="Os Dez Mandamentos">Os Dez Mandamentos</a></i> (English: <span lang="en">'Moses and the Ten Commandments'</span>).
</p><p>In the 2013 Miniseries <i>The Bible</i>, he is portrayed by Stewart Scudamore.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<p>Ramesses is the basis for <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a>'s poem "<a href="/wiki/Ozymandias" title="Ozymandias">Ozymandias</a>". <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> gives an inscription on the base of one of his sculptures as: "<a href="/wiki/King_of_Kings" title="King of Kings">King of Kings</a> am I, Osymandias. If anyone would know how great I am and where I lie, let him surpass one of my works."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> This is paraphrased in Shelley's poem.
</p><p>The life of Ramesses II has inspired many fictional representations, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_novel" title="Historical novel">historical novels</a> of the French writer <a href="/wiki/Christian_Jacq" title="Christian Jacq">Christian Jacq</a>, the <i>Ramsès</i> series; the graphic novel <i><a href="/wiki/Watchmen" title="Watchmen">Watchmen</a></i>, in which the character of Adrian Veidt uses Ramesses II to form part of the inspiration for his alter-ego, <a href="/wiki/Ozymandias_(Watchmen)" title="Ozymandias (Watchmen)">Ozymandias</a>; <a href="/wiki/Norman_Mailer" title="Norman Mailer">Norman Mailer</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Evenings" title="Ancient Evenings">Ancient Evenings</a></i>, which is largely concerned with the life of Ramesses II, though from the perspective of Egyptians living during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_IX" title="Ramesses IX">Ramesses IX</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Anne_Rice" title="Anne Rice">Anne Rice</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mummy,_or_Ramses_the_Damned" title="The Mummy, or Ramses the Damned">The Mummy, or Ramses the Damned</a></i> (1989), in which Ramesses was the main character. In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kane_Chronicles" title="The Kane Chronicles">The Kane Chronicles</a></i> Ramesses is an ancestor of the main characters Sadie and Carter Kane. Ramesses II is one of the characters in the video game <i><a href="/wiki/Civilization_V" title="Civilization V">Civilization V</a></i>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/East_Village,_Manhattan" title="East Village, Manhattan">East Village</a> underground rock band <a href="/wiki/The_Fugs" title="The Fugs">The Fugs</a> released their song "Ramses II Is Dead, My Love" on their 1968 album <i><a href="/wiki/It_Crawled_into_My_Hand,_Honest" title="It Crawled into My Hand, Honest">It Crawled into My Hand, Honest</a></i>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Parisse-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Parisse_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Parisse_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFParisse2021">Parisse, Emmanuel (5 April 2021). <a class="external text" href="https://www.sciencealert.com/22-ancient-pharaohs-have-been-carried-across-cairo-in-an-epic-golden-parade" rel="nofollow">"22 Ancient Pharaohs Have Been Carried Across Cairo in an Epic 'Golden Parade'<span class="cs1-kern-right"></span>"</a>. <i>ScienceAlert</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20220327201021/https://www.sciencealert.com/22-ancient-pharaohs-have-been-carried-across-cairo-in-an-epic-golden-parade" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 27 March 2022<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-Gabriel6-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gabriel6_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGabriel2002">Gabriel, R. (2002). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=y1ngxn_xTOIC" rel="nofollow"><i>The Great Armies of Antiquity</i></a>. Greenwood Publishing Group. p. 6. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780275978099" title="Special:BookSources/9780275978099"><bdi>9780275978099</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Great+Armies+of+Antiquity&amp;rft.pages=6&amp;rft.pub=Greenwood+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=9780275978099&amp;rft.aulast=Gabriel&amp;rft.aufirst=R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dy1ngxn_xTOIC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARamesses+II"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992250–253-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1992250–253_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGrimal1992">Grimal (1992)</a>, pp. 250–253.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDrews1995">Drews (1995)</a>, p. 54<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvp error: no target: CITEREFDrews1995 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span>: "Already in the 1840s Egyptologists had debated the identity of the "northerners, coming from all lands," who assisted the Libyan King Meryre in his attack upon Merneptah. Some scholars believed that Meryre's auxiliaries were merely his neighbors on the Libyan coast, while others identified them as Indo-Europeans from north of the Caucasus. It was one of Maspero's most illustrious predecessors, Emmanuel de Rougé, who proposed that the names reflected the lands of the northern Mediterranean: the Lukka, Ekwesh, Tursha, Shekelesh, and Shardana were men from <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaea" title="Achaea">Achaea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrsenia" title="Tyrsenia">Tyrsenia</a> (western Italy), <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>." De Rougé and others regarded Meryre's auxiliaries—these "peoples de la mer Méditerranée"—as mercenary bands, since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sardinians" title="Sardinians">Sardinians</a>, at least, were known to have served as mercenaries already in the early years of Ramesses the Great. Thus the only "migration" that the Karnak Inscription seemed to suggest was an attempted encroachment by Libyans upon neighboring territory."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-HC20080514-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-HC20080514_27-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation audio-visual cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080416015854/http://www.history.com/shows.do?action=detail&amp;episodeId=211104" rel="nofollow"><i>Ancient Discoveries: Egyptian Warfare</i></a>. Some of these techniques would be used for the very first time in history and some of the battles they fought were on a truly massive scale.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Ancient+Discoveries%3A+Egyptian+Warfare&amp;rft.place=History+Channel+Program%3A+Ancient+Discoveries%3A+Egyptian+Warfare+with+panel+of+three+experts&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.history.com%2Fshows.do%3Faction%3Ddetail%26episodeId%3D211104&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARamesses+II"></span></span>
